Why did they need the USC marching band for the title song? When I heard Fleetwood Mac live touring for this record oh-so-many years ago, the brass section was handled by Christine McVie. Playing an accordion! And it was awesome. That sort of lighter touch would've helped on the LP. Of course, properly focused down to a single LP, the high point of the record, \\"Not That Funny,\\" might've been omitted. But on \\"Tusk,\\" the Christine McVie songs are not that strong. But, as they say, blood is thicker than water, and when Caleb Fallowill sings he'd \\"take one in the temple for you\\" (on \\"Temple\\"), he could easily be talking about the relationship he's got with his fellow bandmates. They're back to bringing out the best in each other, including some very tasty guitar on \\"Coming Back Again\\" from Matthew Followill, who is in top form throughout \\"Mechanical Bull.\\" Be it the arena sized soloing (\\"Rock City\\") or even just using it for subtle coloration (\\"Beautiful War\\"), he's become a force to be reckoned with.<br /><br />\\"Mechanical Bull\\" revels in that diversity. Between the aforementioned strings, the pedal steel on \\"On The Chin,\\" and the album's leaner feel, Kings of Leon are finding their way to greener pastures. And that's no Bull.

Here on his recording he evokes the musical world of Caffarelli with the young and dynamic orchestra Il Pomo D'Oro under its director Riccardo Minasi.<br /><br />Caffarelli (born Gaetano Majorana) was, with Farinelli, the most famous castrato in operatic history. His vocal and interpretative brilliance - and his mercurial temperament - fascinated the audiences of his time: For each of his musical triumphs there was a story of artistic rivalries and eccentric escapades. Over his sometimes stormy career he amassed a fortune and was honored with the title of Duke of San Dorato. Caffarelli created roles in Handel's Serse et Faramondo and it was for him that the composer wrote Serse's celebrated aria `Ombra mai fu'.The album from Na√Øve Records includes a  full-color 76-page booklet with essays and pictures about the music life in Naples, Caffarelli and Farinelli.<br />There is little to say except a sigh of awe in the prformances here recorded. 8866137626|3.0|It's been suggested (here and elsewhere) that Moleskine/Evernote consider marketing the tagging stickers separately from the notebooks. Great idea, but I doubt it will happen, and here's why: the sticker recognition only works when the stickers are applied to the special Evernote Moleskine pages, with either the dotted lines or dotted grid.<br /><br />As an experiment I tried applying stickers to regular Moleskine notebook pages and to plain notebook pages and the app didn't recognise them and wouldn't auto-tag. The app is clearly designed to look for the sticker *and* the special dotted lines. So all the evidence suggests that this is a carefully constructed closed ecosystem. (And the advantage to both companies in promoting loyalty to two products/services is pretty clear.) So users can forget about using/reusing the tagging stickers on their other regular Moleskine notebooks or on other documents. Manual tagging and indexing only for these things.<br /><br />Embracing this system as an existing Moleskine user, therefore, means switching from your previously preferred notebook. (And if you like plain, unlined pages, that means you're out of luck. You're also out of luck if you use several different Moleskines, perhaps mixing the classic notebooks with Cahiers and Volants.) Personally, I like grid pages and the dotted grid, while not quite as refined as the solid line grid, suits me ok. What I'm less keen on is the obvious branding of the embossed cover, which is a bit cheesy with all its clip-art style images. Not classy or \\"timeless\\" in the Moleskine tradition.<br /><br />To be blunt, the Evernote iOS apps work just fine scanning pages from most documents and notebooks. Use dark ink and photograph the pages carefully on a dark surface and you'll be fine. Yes, you'll have to manually move those images to notebooks and assign the tags you want. But you'd be doing that with any note you created in the app anyway.<br /><br />So what does the special notebook offer:<br />1. Sheets of tagging stickers - which do work well to automatically tag and move photographed pages to folders, but only when used in the special notebook. Really, they're a gimmick, because the true value lies in...<br />2. Three months subscription to the Evernote Premium service - in my neck of the woods, that's valued at AUD15.00, which means I save AUD8.00 off the cost of purchasing a regular grid notebook and Evernote Premium service separately.<br /><br />In short, providing you're a dedicated Moleskine user who fills a classic notebook every three months *and* you are taking full advantage of Evernote Premium, this product combo is excellent value.<br /><br />In my case - because I use several different Moleskines - I find each notebook lasts me quite a bit longer than three months - closer to a year. And I'm still trying Evernote Premium (I moved to Premium as a result of the notebook purchase, which is no doubt exactly what Evernote intends from its side of this partnership). I'm not sure I really need what it offers in terms of space/uploads/additional services. So if I find I'm not exploiting it, I'll be back to the free Evernote service and regular Moleskines, sans cheesy embossed covers.

But even while doing this, he doubts his own sanity (his mother was institutionalized for schizophrenia many years ago), and visits a counselor. His wife and brother and co-workers become very concerned about him, but he can't be dissuaded.<br /><br />The plot moves along at a slow pace with some intense emotional scenes well acted by Michael Shannon as Curtis and Jessica Chastain as his wife. A sense of doom pervades the plot, as you fear that Curtis' irrational actions are going to spiral into madness and ruin his family life and marriage. And he can't seem to stop it. After he builds the storm shelter, there is a storm warning and they spend the night in the shelter, but it's not a major storm. The family eventually takes their summer vacation at a beach house at the urging of a psychiatrist that Curtis visits, and all seems normal until the final scenes of the movie. A menacing storm arrives and the movie ends... everything unresolved, leaving you to make up your own ending. I liked the movie well enough right up until the ending, but then I couldn't figure out what I thought about it.... Thought provoking and complex - I'll be thinking about this movie for a while.

B00ARHAAZ6|4.0|Steelheart is a bit different than Sanderson's previous work, but not in a bad way.  It's basically a really, REALLY good movie screenplay.  Concepts, background, characters are all distilled into a very concentrated, very potent form.  This is used in the best fashion, to let the worldbuilding (that Sanderson is incredibly good at, evinced by his other stories) fall a bit to the wayside and get to the action ASAP.  Many will say that it uses tropes a bit too much, using archetypes to make you \\"know\\" a character without so much character development, but I see this as serving a greater goal.  Like a comic book (and given the series setting, this is infinitely appropriate) it paints in broad strokes the background so that the pacing of the story is kept tight and taught.  It also has a second, meta-contextual level that makes it a subtle commentary on both humanity, as well as our fascination with the super-hero/villain and the power-fantasy.  Who are the heroes in this story?  Who, the villains?  And what, ultimately, is the differentiation?  Steelheart poses all these questions to the reader, I'm fascinated by where Sanderson is going with this?  The only drawback is that the series isn't done yet!  And isn't a film(s)!  Hollywood/HBO, THIS is your next blockbuster (doesn't hurt that much of the action revolves around \\"ordinary\\" humans, might keep the VFX budget a bit under control.

B00D2ZF3GQ|3.0|Upon it's release, I initially avoided After Earth like I would a pack of rabid baboons. Terrible critic reviews and middling audience feedback gave me the impression that this promising-sounding sci-fi was another one of M. Night Shyamalan's recent streak of bad films.<br /><br />When I actually watched it on DVD (thankfully, on our HD TV), I expected the ABSOLUTE worst, but was pleasantly surprised to find that the movie was NOT a miserable trainwreck. It wasn't great, wasn't classic. Wasn't even entirely awesome. But, as I begrudgingly had to admit, After Earth was the first watchable Shyamalan movie since Signs, nd not a *bad* movie...<br /><br />That having been said, the movie had flaws big enough to drive Shyamalan's ego through. The dialogue was *absolutely* cringeworthy. Laughably serious, terribly mundane, and cliched in that way that only pulp sci-fi can be (\\"It has found you.\\"), it had that joyless quality that only Shyamalan can bring. The acting was little better.<br /><br />Will Smith is dead serious, and as stern and commanding as the world's most humorless general. The gravitas was still there (That \\"Take a knee\\" line is strangely moving), but without his usual charm and charisma, Smith Sr. seemed to drag the movie down like a guy who had both his legs broken. Oh, right...<br />Jaden has no real reason to be here (other than his father being in the same film), and we all hoped for a stronger actor, but he managed to be....watchable. Juuuust watchable, not extraordinary in any way. Though there were scenes, such as his climactic fight with the Ursa, where just a little of his father's talent and screen presence can be viewed in him.<br /><br />Other cons include the plodding pace the movie accepts at times, and the looong time it takes to get off the ground (literally). And the \\"What the--?\\" accent everyone is using. No, seriously: What the--?!<br /><br />...<br /><br />But prepare to be amazed when Shyamalan just shows you amazing stuff: a group of men jogging underneath a bloodred sun, herd of bison roaming over hills, a lush forest under the sky, a volcano floating flakes of ash down from the sky, a troop of monkeys running through a rain forest. The CGI was not the best I've ever seen, but the creature design and amazing cinematography really made up for that.<br /><br />As a huge animal lover, I was majorly entertained any time some wierd and wonderful animal came onscreen. The fact that sabretooths and giant vultures exist is absolutely ludicrous, but ludicrous in that suspend-your-disbelief-and-take-it-for-what-it-is way. Love the monkeys, but weren't there okapi's in the trailer?<br /><br />The landscape itself became a supporting character (again - the whole rainforest freezing thing is ludicrous, but passable). The cinematography was, at times extraordinary. That volcano fight at the end was gorgeously shot, and, I am ashamed to say, somewhat moving (being the only moment in the After Earth that I could actually label \\"cool\\" or \\"awesome\\"). The biggest surprise for me was the score by James Newton Howard, which made sit up straight and go \\"Whaaaat? This movie has a good score? I gotta get this on iTunes!\\" And mention has to go to the costumes and tech, and for being the first sci-fi movie that has wood and cloth interiors rather than chrome and glass, which was pleasantly original.<br /><br />And hey, that speech about danger and fear is some profound stuff, man.<br /><br />...<br /><br />Conclusion:<br />CONS: Dialogue abysmal and laughable. Acting joyless and stiff. Story unoriginal and pace plodding.<br />PROS: Stunning visuals, amazing cinematography, excellent score, costumes, and set design.<br />My rating? I give After Earth a solid 3 stars - not great, not awful. All that negative reception is understandable, but not wholly warranted. This is not a classic for the ages, but rather, a pleasantly enjoyable night at the movies!<br /><br />P.S. B00BC24NT4|5.0|Forget the Russians, Chinese or even terrorists: in &#34;A Delicate Truth,&#34; his latest espionage thriller, John Le Carr&eacute; (&#34;Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy&#34;) conjures a world in which mercenaries and their weak-kneed enablers in the government turn dishonest dollars  based on lethal lies and misrepresentation..<br /><br />It used to be good guys versus bad guys in Le Carr&eacute;'s fictional universe. At times there wasn't much separating them, but at least they were working against each other. In his craven new post-Iron curtain universe, almost everybody is a bad guy -- even the people who served as heroes in his earlier works.<br /><br />The novel's plot is sordid and anything but simple: the &#34;delicate truth,&#34; of the title is the counterpart to the official lies used to cover up an intelligence catastrophe that occurred on the British colony of Gibraltar during the Gordon Brown era. The disaster occurs when a team of British special ops soldiers are assigned to snatch a terrorist who is supposedly temporarily hiding on the island. Although British troops are used for the op, control over it has been ceded to a private intelligence-for-hire company by the British government.<br /><br />As it turns out, however, the corrupt U.S. firm is only interested in collecting Her Majesty's paycheck for the job: the terrorist has actually never been on the island. Two innocents are accidentally murdered during the botched kidnapping..<br /><br />Despite the screw-up, the operation is termed a success and everyone involved is either paid off or discredited. One of them is Kitt Probyn, a past-his-prime Foreign Office flunkie who was assigned to oversee the scheme because the British military would not commit combat troops to an operation that was not putatively under the control of the foreign office. Probyn, a kindly and patriotic bureaucrat, is far out of his depth, a perfect foil for the plot's real masterminds.<br /><br />Probyn is posted to a posh Foreign Service sinecure in the Caribbean and then pensioned off with an honorary title designed to keep him from discussing the Gibraltar incident. Meanwhile, a more astute foreign service operative named Toby Bell launches his own completely unofficial investigation into the kidnapping and its aftermath..<br /><br />Working separately, the two men meet through happenstance and piece together the real story of what happened on Gibraltar, but the novel reaches its cynical climax leaving readers uncertain whether they succeed in making &#34;the delicate truth&#34; public or are themselves victims of government suppression.<br /><br />The story is vintage Le Carr&eacute; with characters whose personalities are so clearly portrayed you can almost visualize them in your mind's eye. The plot, given the recent history of botched intelligence operations, the use of mercenaries as an adjunct to official military force, and the reliance on mendacity by governmental policy makers, seems like something you might read about in your morning paper.<br /><br />The &#34;Delicate Truth&#34; is, this novel is Le Carr&eacute;'s way of warning us that our political leaders are taking us down the garden path and we shouldn't trust them for a minute. It is clear the master spy writer is mad as hell about the way the lines have merged between government policy and private money-making schemes. &#34;The Delicate Truth&#34; is a hell of a read and Le Carr&eacute; has done us a favor by writing it.e sinecure in the Caribbean and then pensioned off with an honorary title designed to keep him from discussing the Gibraltar incident. 1781162646|4.0|Stephen King is not primarily known as a mystery writer. Nonetheless, when he writes a whodunit, he writes a good one. Such is the case with &#34;Joyland,&#34; released as a Hard Case Crime imprint in June.<br /><br />Devin Jones, a college student, takes a summer job at Joyland, a beachfront North Carolina amusement park that has seen better days. Jones, a down-and-out scholar who needs summer employment to continue his education, answers a help-wanted ad and ends up literally spending the summer as a &#34;dog's body,&#34; one of the general maintenance &#34;helpers&#34; who keep the place going.<br /><br />During Devin's first visit to the park he learns Joyland is supposedly haunted by the ghost of a young woman whose throat was slashed by a mysterious attacker in a creepy ride that is often used as a make-out spot by Joyland's teenaged patrons. The crime remains unsolved four years later and Devin takes it on as a sideline to his maintenance work.<br /><br />By the final pages, Devin gathers enough clues to unmask the villain. The solution to the mystery is not one of those cheapies where key facts are withheld until the bad guy is revealed: King arranges all the evidence as if challenging his readers to identify the culprit.<br /><br />But &#34;Joyland&#34; is not one of those novels where unmasking the villain is a critical part of the fun. Like one of the amusements at the park, the best part of &#34;Joyland&#34; is sitting back and enjoying the ride.<br /><br />All the characters in are deftly sketched and portrayed with a vivid and loving touch. Even relatively minor figures get King's undivided attention. And he makes good use of carny lingo to add believability to his characters as members of a credible subculture.<br /><br />Part of the book's charm is that it leaves the reader with the sense that he or she actually knows these people. King manages to pack more than a summer's worth of action into less than 300 pages, yet readers will finish the book wishing for more.<br /><br />-- William E. Wallacehing for more.<br /><br />-- William E. Wallace
1476727651|4.0|I'm assuming, like most King fans (hopefully), I was a little skeptical when I heard he was writing a sequel to THE SHINING.  The Stephen King of today is not the Stephen King of yesteryear; in most aspects, this is a good thing (first and foremost, he probably wouldn't still be alive), but there's no denying that his novels have changed.  For the better or worse, that's up to you (and I think it depends on the individual works); but the fact is, today's Stephen King would never have written THE SHINING in the exact same way.<br /><br />DOCTOR SLEEP got my hopes up after I started reading it.  The first 100 pages are brilliant; they're among the best King has given us recently, and fit perfectly with THE SHINING.  But as soon as Danny starts to mature, the more matured King settles in, and we're thrown into a meandering tale in which, surprisingly, there don't seem to be any real stakes; not once (outside of those first 100 pages) did I feel any of the characters were in any real danger.  King seems more focused on building character depth, which he's become fairly great at; but his characterization gets in the way of the story, slowing down the plot (remember 11/22/63, which could've done without roughly 300 pages and made a lot more sense?).<br /><br />As for plot: enter Dan Torrence.  One winter long ago, Danny and his  parents spent the winter at the Overlook Hotel.  They weren't alone there, however, and Danny's father succumbed to alcoholism and supernatural forces.  Fast forward to the present (with a detour through Danny's childhood, hence SLEEP's brilliant beginning), and Dan has hit rock bottom.  He's his father's son, a struggling alcoholic with a violent temper.  He finds his personal low...and shortly thereafter, he finds a second chance in a small New Hampshire town.  He also finds a strange pen pal: a young girl named Abra, who has the shining and has it bad.  So much so, in fact, that she's become the target of a group of psychic vampires: the True Knot, led by the sadistic Rose the Hat, who tortures children with the shining, thus gaining something pretty darn close to immortality.  The True Knot are moving in on Abra...and thanks to a third-act twist that is hardly necessary, Dan may be the only person who can help keep Abra alive.<br /><br />Except...like I said earlier: not once do you ever stop and think, \\"Huh, this may not end well for everyone involved.\\"  Part of it is King's devotion to his characters.  He goes so far out of his way to humanize everyone, you don't really see the True Knot as a real threat (except for a key early scene).  As with 11/22/63, DOCTOR SLEEP goes on too long.  It could easily have ended as soon as Abra hit her early adolescent years (no spoilers).  Instead, King draws the story out.  We get some great characters in the process, but the plot suffers.  DOCTOR SLEEP is still worthy of a spot in your collection; but it pales painfully when compared to its predecessor.  The worst sin a sequel can make is not justifying its existence as a sequel; SLEEP could've existed without Dan Torrence, since after the book gets started it's not even about him.  However, if you step back from its origins and ignore some glaring plot holes, DOCTOR SLEEP is a decent read.  Not the best King has given us, not even the best lately, but Constant Readers will still enjoy it.not, led by the sadistic Rose the Hat, who tortures children with the shining, thus gaining something pretty darn close to immortality. He goes so far out of his way to humanize everyone, you don't really see the True Knot as a real threat (except for a key early scene).  As with 11/22/63, DOCTOR SLEEP goes on too long.  It could easily have ended as soon as Abra hit her early adolescent years (no spoilers).  Instead, King draws the story out.  We get some great characters in the process, but the plot suffers.  DOCTOR SLEEP is still worthy of a spot in your collection; but it pales painfully when compared to its predecessor.  The worst sin a sequel can make is not justifying its existence as a sequel; SLEEP could've existed without Dan Torrence, since after the book gets started it's not even about him.  However, if you step back from its origins and ignore some glaring plot holes, DOCTOR SLEEP is a decent read.  Not the best King has given us, not even the best lately, but Constant Readers will still enjoy it.

Mostly a mishmash of BLADE RUNNER, A.I. and every antihero cop movie ever made, the film follows Detective Del Spooner (Will Smith) on a quest to find the killer of Dr. Alfred Lanning, the genius behind the workforce-dominating humanoid robots that fill up nearly every scene in this clunky CGI/live amalgamation. The film supposedly takes place in Chicago, but there is not one slight reminder of that city until a shot of the river near the very end that's vaguely recognizable. Spooner hates robots and suspects them for some reason from the beginning, but we never get to know him well enough to care; the film spends much more time erotically fascinated by Will Smith's big pecs than it does in creating a cohesive person for him to play.<br /><br />Of course, robots aren't people; robots can't kill, or so we are told. There are many quick, fleeting references to interesting themes throughout the film such as:<br />* what does it mean to be human?<br />* is hating a harmless machine similar to being racist (both Spooner and his long-suffering boss are black, in a world that seems to have nearly, but not quite, forgotten that fact)?<br />* at one point does a human with replacement parts start to become more machine than man * does it emasculate, or ameliorate humanity to have its menial tasks performed by something man-created?<br />* can you actually program a machine to think for itself?<br /><br />etc etc...but they are all subordinated to endless chases and explosions and gun battles. There are no characters of consequence here, and even the seeming possibility of a love story between Spooner and scientist Susan Calvin (based on one of Asimov's most famous characters but unrecognizable apart from the name) goes nowhere. There just isn't time with all the action they have to put in! Not to mention dozens of product placements, and, did I forget to mention, Will Smith's perfect-as-a-robot sculpted body. In fact, given the way the principle actors seem to sleepwalk through this film, the most interesting idea I was left with was a question as to who and what was most robotic in the film; was there, in fact, any human touch to the movie at all?of Asimov's most famous characters but unrecognizable apart from the name) goes nowhere. B00D8HNR2K|4.0|I was first introduced to Nine Inch Nails through an acquaintance in high school. He told me I needed to listen to his copy of \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\" in the middle of 1990. At the time I thought Mr. Reznor was doing something few others were doing. Of course, in retrospect, I know he was working a similar vein as Ministry, Skinny Puppy and Front 242, but I wasn't familiar with those bands at the time, and so \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\" sounded like something I hadn't really been exposed to. Still, I held back, didn't buy a copy of \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\", but filed Mr. Reznor away in the back of my mind as someone to pay attention to in the future.<br /><br />The \\"Broken\\" EP didn't register to me, and so it wasn't until 1994's \\"The Downward Spiral\\" that I really paid close attention. The video for \\"Closer\\" grabbing me with its disturbing scenes and dark imagery. \\"The Downward Spiral\\" was nothing like anything I had heard before, not even like what Nirvana, Pearl Jam or Soundgarden were doing at that time. I bought \\"The Downward Spiral\\" eventually along with \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\" and \\"Broken\\". I also remember my freshman year of college (1991) that I could hear songs from \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\" blasting from various campus dorm rooms like some kind of rebellious mantra.<br /><br />And so, several albums later, and many years later, here I sit with my copy of \\"Hesitation Marks\\". I remember when Mr. Reznor toured and then pulled the plug on Nine Inch Nails. I remember thinking it seemed premature. And so, five years later, here I am with \\"Hesitation Marks\\".<br /><br />Like all Nine Inch Nail albums, I know I have to repeatedly listen, letting the music just saturate my mind, letting the soundscapes and moods drift in and out of my consciousness before I pass judgment and write my review. Although I like Nine Inch Nails, I wouldn't classify them (him, Mr. Reznor) in my top favorites of all time, but there has consistently been an interest in whatever Mr. Reznor does with Nine Inch Nails over the years to where I own most all of his music under that moniker.<br /><br />I know I will get hate mail and negative votes for this, but I am going to type it anyway. I liked \\"Hesitation Marks\\". I liked it a lot. I listened to all of Nine Inch Nails previous albums throughout this week as well, for comparison. \\"Hesitation Marks\\", for me, is a very good album. I like what Mr. Reznor is doing here. Working with Lindsey Buckingham (Fleetwood Mac) and Adrian Belew (Paul Simon, Frank Zappa, David Bowie, Talking Heads, Kind Crimson), two of my favorite experimental guitarists. I felt \\"Hesitation Marks\\" was well crafted, meticulously so, and mature for Mr. Reznor. Whereas in the past he could be all angst with noisy buzz saws and quiet, reflective moments, here, on \\"Hesitation Marks\\" he restrains himself. \\"Hesitation Marks\\" is a master craftsman carving out the highest quality furniture you could possibly buy--solid mahogany and sturdy oak. This is album Mr. Reznor (age 48, mind you) has been threatening to make--an album that sums up all the best elements of Nine Inch Nails.<br /><br />I wanted to give it five stars, but I restrained my self, knowing five stars would mean it's equal to, or greater than \\"Pretty Hate Machine\\" and \\"The Downward Spiral\\"--two seminal, classic albums by today's standards. Yet, this could be right there with those two albums if, with time, listeners soften and realize how well done it truly is.<br /><br />I found myself going over the tracks, looking for weak ones, and struggling to find them, but I guess, perhaps, \\"Various Methods Of Escape\\" might qualify, but that's track number nine. The first eight tracks are virtually seamless in their quality and track order. \\"Copy Of A\\", \\"Came Back Haunted\\", \\"Find My Way\\", \\"All Time Low\\", \\"Disappointed\\", \\"Everything\\" and \\"Satellite\\" all fit well together, varying their degrees of sound and rhythm with thought-provoking lyrics. \\"Came Back Haunted\\" might be one of my favorite tracks here. Thelast four tracks \\"Running\\", \\"I Would For You\\", \\"In Two\\" and \\"While I'm Still Here\\" finish off the album, with some interesting turns by Lindsey Buckingham, particularly on \\"While I'm Still Here\\". If you, like me, have been a long time fan of Adrian Belew and Lindsey Buckingham, you can hear their influences throughout, but also hear they are trying different things here, things not found on their solo works, or works with Fleetwood Mac and King Crimson.<br /><br />So, I'm not really sure why fans would be disappointed by \\"Hesitation Marks\\". Especially fans who, like me, have been listening to Nine Inch Nails from the beginning. This is a mature, evolved piece of music that you can listen to from front to back without hitting the skip button. That's a feat in today's cannibalized market. Whereas, Mr. Reznor's earlier works were demarcated by jagged juxtapositions of harsh blasts of industrial angst punctuated by nearly inaudible moments of reflective moodiness, \\"Hesitation Marks\\" sands down the wild mood swings of yesteryear and replaces it with a sophistication Mr. Reznor could not have achieved in his more youthful form. Again, for someone who is 48, \\"Hesitation Marks\\" shows no signs of aging. It is remarkable that Mr. Reznor left for five years and delivered one of his most thought-provoking works in years.<br /><br />So here is how \\"Hesitation Marks\\" compares with Nine Inch Nails previous works:<br /><br />1989 Pretty Hate Machine: Five Stars<br />1994 The Downward Spiral: Five Stars<br />1999 The Fragile: Three Stars (a double album that would have been much better distilled into one album that might have been five stars)<br />2005 With Teeth: Three and a Half Stars<br />2007 Year Zero: Three and a Half Stars<br />2008 The Slip: Three Stars (I don't count the instrumental \\"Ghosts I-IV\\" as a legitimate album, even though I know it is)<br />2013 Hesitation Marks: Four and a Half Starslast four tracks \\"Running\\", \\"I Would For You\\", \\"In Two\\" and \\"While I'm Still Here\\" finish off the album, with some interesting turns by Lindsey Buckingham, particularly on \\"While I'm Still Here\\". Among those belongings is a crystal that will change everything...<br /><br />When Jesse sets out with her friend Marcus to solve the mystery of her mother's disappearance, the plot kicks into high gear. B00C9QQBA6|4.0|I very much enjoyed AURORA CONSPIRACY THE SERIES: CRASH LANDING - EPISODE 1.  I'm not much of a scifi or fantasy fan, but I thought this story was exciting and entertaining. I particularly enjoyed the contrast between the time setting - 1897 - and the crashed-spaceship situation. I couldn't help but think this was the \\"Cowboys and Aliens\\" movie that SHOULD have been made!<br /><br />The characters are defined primarily by their actions and dialog. Except for a short first chapter introducing us to the aliens, their history, and their current dilemma, there was not a lot of exposition, which I thought helped move this action-and-romance story along very well.<br /><br />This quick plunge into action and adventure in CRASH LANDING was a plus for me. I find it awfully difficult to wade through scifi stories that immediately overwhelm the reader with page upon page of dense mythology. Painstakingly crafted and beloved by the author as that mythology might be, it's very daunting to a non-fanboy. So many scifi books begin with something like, \\"For millions of millennia the High Inscrutable Scrimshaw of the Cranberrusians had kept Vice-Emperor Zurg of the Grilledcheez Command safe from the treachery of Mudbathians, who defeated the Guineapygs to colonize the third moon of Hopscotch in order to secure the Stone of Anthrax, which held all the power of Zurg's ancestors, the Dingbats.\\"  Thank goodness, authors Ginger Gelsheimer and Christina Keats wisely avoided this pitfall. We get just enough information to realize who and what we're dealing with, then the story is off and running.<br /><br />I thought the characters were fresh and original, and even the alien hero is comfortingly human, despite his otherworldly origins. Emily and Aurelius are instantly likeable, and it's very easy to root for them to overcome what seem like insurmountable problems.<br /><br />I did notice anachronistic dialog. The likes of \\"Gotcha,\\" \\"You're not kidding,\\" and \\"Big Bad Space Man\\" pop up hereand there, but that really wasn't a problem for me. Han, Luke, and Leia talk that way, don't they? It's scifi adventure, after all, not Regency romance or Napoleonic High Drama.<br /><br />It should be noted that CRASH LANDING is not a complete novel. It is, as it claims to be right up front, the first episode of a serial story.  Novella-length, it's a quick and entertaining read. It does end mid-action with a cliffhanger, so be prepared to stock up on the next episodes as well to avoid nail-biting frustration. The stories are reasonably priced to accommodate this.<br /><br />All in all, this story is well-written and a lot of fun!

B00DW5IM9Q|5.0|I don't know much, but I do know Dylan (not the man, only his art and career which is just fine by me).  Many, perhaps most, of the folks who rank themselves among his core fans will never appreciate this release.  The sad truth is that Dylan's breadth of interests and genres exceeds that of virtually all of his fans, myself included.  That's a real shame as this particular Dylan release not only contains some his greatest songs but also some really nice covers of both other artists' and his own prior work.  This all comes from a period when Dylan was actively trying to deconstruct his image and to disappear from public scrutiny.  He documented his perspective at that fairly well in his \\"Chronicles, Volume 1\\" autobiography.  This release is a split between what is essentially a different release of \\"New Morning\\" and better material from his \\"Self Portrait\\" and \\"Dylan\\" cover albums plus a few outtakes from Nashville Skyline.    My starting perspective was that I generally like this era and have long considered \\"New Morning\\" to be one of Dylan's top releases.  However, I cared little for \\"Self Portrait\\" and \\"Dylan\\" save for a few scattered gems.  In order to get to an ideal frame of mind to review this I avoided all liner notes and sequestered myself and my wife for two weeks on an almost vacant beach.  I took along \\"Chronicles Vol 1\\" and studied it along with the Wikipedia Dylan LP ring from \\"John Westley Harding\\" through \\"New Morning\\".  I brought along all released material from that era including some Bootleg Series outtakes from the period with George Harrison and Johnny Cash.  What? Am I some rich retired millionaire who can simply fly off and dedicate this kind of effort to a review?  Nope.  We were just a harried and harassed couple, sick of our jobs and subject to an unexpected stream of negative chaotic events over the past 2 years.  We had arranged for a badly needed off season vacation with a mindset to hide from the world's cross hairs, much as Dylan and his family were doing in these years albeit for much better reasons and on a grand scale.  Still, we were in that mode in our little world and this release came out just as we were to depart.  Upon realizing that Amazon allowed immediate mp3 download I grabbed it along with Chronicles on the way out the door.  I've just picked up the physical package 3 weeks later, but I'll crack the shrink wrap after I review to avoid any influence from the liner notes.  Have I mentioned that I'm in an ideal state of mind to review this?    Without exception, all of this material is better than or equal to the previously released versions.  Here we find Dylan in great voice, and it is his natural voice without any affect whatsoever.  Critics have written that he had an extra octave on New Morning and indeed Dylan has claimed that his voice throughout this era was sweet from quitting smoking.  Not so. That's typical Dylan media manipulation and it's patently absurd to think that smoking cessation could change the voice of a young man in his early 30's to that degree, particularly as the main difference is far more stylistic than tonal in nature.  Further, Dylan had an additional octave beyond this material on his very first release as clearly demonstrated on the yodeling in the \\"Freight Train Blues\\" recording.  Shift forward 3 or 4 decades and we have the croaking Dylan of the last 5 releases, the raspy Dylan DJ of Theme Time Radio Hour and the wheezing, creaking Dylan of the Mike Wallace 60 Minutes interview.  I just have to laugh as we also have recent recorded evidence of a silky smooth Dylan speaking voice.  \\"Manipulator of crowds\\" indeed. The fact is that Dylan's vocal command has always been nearly unrivaled and this hasn't changed.  Outside of Billie Holiday I have yet to hear another vocalist who could match Dylan's ability to intensively focus to the point of giving every single syllable on \\"Blonde on Blonde\\", \\"Highway 61\\" and \\"Blood on the Tracks\\" its own special consideredtreatment.  In this era, he chooses to treat honest folk and country songs with an honest voice.  That's an artist's choice, a good one in my opinion and much of the reason why the casual radio listening public generally enjoyed the best known work of this period, \\"Lay Lady Lay\\", without any of the usual complaints about \\"that awful Dylan voice\\".    New Morning - 5 stars all the way    To me the \\"New Morning\\" material here is what \\"New Morning\\" should have been.  In \\"Chronicles Vol 1\\" Dylan wrote of how the press was waiting for his next move, his next vision, denied that he's the voice of a generation, etc all of which is pretty true.  He then sequed to a car ride conversation with Robbie Robertson of the Band where Robertson asks \\"So, where do you think you're going to take it [the music industry] next?\\" and Dylan claims that he couldn't believe that even Robertson doesn't get it, that Dylan isn't a leader, etc.  But these are two quite different topics and this is merely an example of Dylan's typical word games.  Dylan wasn't the voice of a generation, nor was he a movement leader.  However, along with the Beatles, he undeniably had been setting the musical direction in folk, folk rock and rock for nearly a decade at this point.  In fact, this was exactly the problem, the very role from which he was attempting to extricate himself.  Dylan easily could have taken the music industry in a new direction with \\"New Morning\\", and in my opinion this material proves that he was considering doing exactly that.  I believe that he was wrestling with this decision and that the incredible horn based title track of \\"New Morning\\" as included here could have been a startling genre changing #1 single in 1970.  That of course was exactly what Robbie Robertson was asking about, totally within Dylan's power to do and indeed what he had already been doing for years.    The previously released \\"New Morning\\" title cut is a great song in its own right with a sparse, stripped down acoustic/electric arrangement kicked along with a nice groove by Russell Kunkel's typically stellar drumming.  But this alternate full blown horn based \\"New Morning\\" that we have here is an animal which simply cannot be contained.  It would demand radio play as surely as \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" had in the prior decade  These horns are commanding; alternately brash, ballsy, melodic, triumphant and even humorous as the song calls for it.  The electric and acoustic guitar overdubs that have been added back in are also spectacular.  After 3 solid weeks of listening I still cannot believe how compelling this take is.  All of the other original takes from \\"New Morning\\" included here are similarly better and quite different from the release.    Dylan wouldn't approach this level of orchestration again until he was fully out of hiding in the late '70s \\"Street Legal\\" and throughout the '80s and '90's starting first with the incredible horn Tour de force \\"Brownsville Girl\\".  It is important to realize that when \\"New Morning\\" was released in 1970 it was to a post Woodstock/Altamont/psychedelic era into a directionless vacuum which would not be filled until the mid '70s disco direction.  Had this horn based \\"New Morning\\" been released, we would have had an entirely different 70's scene.  Late 60's/early 70's soul & Motown artists would have quickly added more brass.  David Bromberg's Chicago big band concept (did it start in these \\"New Morning\\" sessions?) would have been bigger and more successful.  The Stones would have added bluesy horns in a flash.. there would have been no Rolling Bee Gee's \\"Emotional Rescue\\" LP.  The 70's would have been very different, far more diverse. New Wave and Rap may well have been avoided... ahhh, well... we all have our dreams.    The alternate takes of \\"Went to See the Gypsy\\" are fascinating.  The originally released Gypsy has this great understated electric guitar which just hangs back throughout the entire tune with very few, always tasteful, jaggedriffs... invariably at just the right time... all just waiting to cut loose at the end.  However, in the end the guitar does kick in and sizzle but it never quite cooks and that is maddening although it really is a great cut.  Here however, we have an early acoustic demo version and I'm going to guess that it's David Bromberg playing lead on it because his playing is as recognizable to me as his face.  He plays melodic riffs and games with Dylan's vocals throughout, but there are gaps in the song... key lyrics and ideas missing.  But it all is just great. The final lyrics on the released version (and here as well on a second great keyboard based Gypsy) is better and details backing \\"He [the Gypsy] did it in Las Vegas, and he can do it here...\\" are more fleshed out.  Maddeningly though, the final lyrics do drop the single best lyric (only heard here): \\"I contemplated every move, or at least I tried\\".  But to be honest, it was dropped as it really didn't fit the rest of the verse.  That's how one can tell that this is an early version as typical Dylan outtakes have substantially different poetry and imagery which arrive at the same meaning.  For example, on \\"Blood on the Tracks\\", \\"Idiot Wind\\" contains \\"I ran into the fortune teller who said beware of lightning that might strike.  I haven't known peace and quiet for so long I can't remember what it's like\\" but the original had \\"I threw the I Ching yesterday, it said there might be some thunder at the well. Peace and quiet's been avoiding me For so long it seems like living hell\\".  Very different, yet the same vibe and meaning.  So we are still waiting for an ultimate version of Gypsy with something akin to the final lyrics but I'll be enjoying the three that we now have for at least the rest of my life.    One song which came off truly badly on \\"New Morning\\" was \\"If Dogs Run Free\\", due to horribly misplaced and discontinuous skat singing which rendered it all but unlistenable.  Here however we have the same backing vocalists with much better yet equally quirky jazz tinged vocal arrangements.  \\"Time Passes Slowly\\" which was beautiful before is even better now, more rhythmic, a little better arrangemed and some great \\"la, la, la-ing\\" vocal work from his Bobness.  And there is still yet another great arrangement of it as well on this same release!  It is just all too much... considering the full impact of what \\"New Morning\\" would have been.  There is also some ghostly keyboard throughout these sessions which to my ear might be the same player who participated in the original New York sessions for \\"Blood on the Tracks\\".    I've left a lot out, there is just so much more to say and tracks that I haven't mentioned.  But this is essential listening.    Self Portrait - I waffled between 4 & 5, but there is no choice.  5 stars    The Self Portrait/Dylan portion of the release is complicated for so many reasons.  In \\"Chronicles Vol 1\\", Dylan notes that he recorded covers, threw them against the wall and released whatever stuck.  But he also writes about how he was intentionally trying to drop out of the public eye and he later told Mike Wallace that he had intentionally made some bad albums in this era.  Although it did contain the beautiful \\"Copper Kettle\\" and a nice take of Paul Simon's \\"The Boxer\\", it is clear that what really \\"stuck\\" for him was a release of lesser takes which could serve to diminish his profile.  That is not so on this release.  Incredibly beautiful takes of tunes such as \\"Pretty Saro\\", \\"Tattle ODay\\", \\"Spanish Is The Loving Tongue\\", \\"Thirsty Boots\\" as well some of his own tunes (\\"I Threw it All Away\\" & \\"When I Paint My Masterpiece\\") are easily as good as anything that he's ever done.    The \\"Self Portrait\\" material is yet further complicated in genre and song selection in that his fans were generally not interested in their protest and electric folk hero moving to a soft middle of the road folk/country genre, let alone doing covers.  True, Dylan had picked up so manyof these tunes in the Gaslight Van Ronk/Peter, Paul and Mary scene followed by the Joan Baez/Eric Anderson London hotel rooms as documented in \\"Don't Look Back\\" but fans of the time weren't clued in to that.  Later early '90s Dylan releases \\"Good As I Been to You\\" and \\"World Gone Wrong\\" were also all covers and far better accepted even though less than a third of the material was well done.  But the original \\"Self Portrait\\" had less than 3 good covers on a double album so you certainly can't blame the fans.  Also, by the '90s people were used to Dylan changing and blues were more palatable to his core audience than folk and country can ever be.    But one insurmountable problem remains with Dylan doing covers.  Simply put, most artists' top signature songs are just not going to rank as top or even second tier Dylan songs.  This is true of \\"The Boxer\\", \\"Thirsty Boots\\" and all of the others here except for a few of the traditional ones that I noted above.  Good as Dylan's covers are, the material simply does not equal his own.  For example, his takes on standards such as \\"Alberta\\", \\"Days of 49\\" & \\"Little Sadie\\" would easily rank as career best signature efforts for country and folk/old time artists ranging from Willie Nelson to Doc Watson.  But in the Dylan pantheon they simply don't rank at all and are now gone from my iPod after 3 weeks as are most of the original \\"Self Portrait\\" versions.    But I can't hold that against Dylan in ranking this release as they are top takes in their genre. B005N9EZ90|1.0|Let me start by saying that my review is in no way an insult towards Diamond Head or their music. The original album is five-star material all this way. This review is exclusively for the 2CD \\"Deluxe Edition.\\"<br /><br />Diamond Head should need no introduction, they were one of the finest bands of the NWOBHM, constantly pleasing audiences and being a massive influence on so many bands, many of whom have become far better known than Diamond Head themselves. Not surprisingly, they are a band whose back catalogue has seen numerous reissues over the past several years.<br /><br />By my count, prior to this release, there were no fewer than three different releases of this album, including a \\"White Album\\" reissue with the original seven album tracks and seven bonus tracks from EPs and singles and the likes, and a \\"Diamond Nights\\" CD with different mixes and a \\"scrambled\\" tracklist of the album and EP material from the era. I was really looking forward to seeing a Deluxe Edition of this material, which would being even more rarities and remastering treatment to the table.<br /><br />...I never would have expected the so-called \\"Deluxe Edition\\" to be the WORST release this album has had to date!<br /><br />All they did was took the same 14 tracks from the \\"White Album\\" release and SPREAD THEM ACROSS TWO DISCS. You heard me right. There is NO ADDITIONAL OR NEW BONUS CONTENT. They just took a one-CD version of this album and spread the tracks across two discs, adding nothing new. Seriously, the record company couldn't hunt down some unreleased outtakes or early live material? In essence, all this release does is takes already-existing releases of the album and makes them more expensive by spreading the content across two discs. There is a ton of wasted blank space that could have been filled with so much unreleased stuff. If ever there was a wasted opportunity, this is it.<br /><br />Long story short - get Lightning to the Nations, but don't get this version. The \\"White Album\\" reissue, containing expanded liner notes and featuring all of this material on ONE CD (the way it should be) is the way to go.

B00EE3FVSU|4.0|As the above, I had heard a lot about this series and looked forward to seeing it.  I was not disappointed, as its 8 hour format enabled it to explore the affect the murder has on the characters and the larger community of a small town on the Coast of Southwestern England.  That part was well done. The scene at the end between the two women most affected by the crime was particularly good and unexpected (at least to me).  The plot was full of the usual obvious red-herrings, but at least they weren't incredible as many are in comparable stories.  And, I think, the identity of the murderer was a genuine surprise. David Tennant's character was yet another tortured detective, whose agonies became a little tiresome as the series progressed (why was he still a policeman?), and who it was increasingly hard to sympathize with especially as I couldn't understand half of what he said (due to his thick Scots accent and mumbling).  In fact, the difficulty in understanding the dialog was my major gripe - not the British accent, but the fact that no one talks into a microphone or enunciates clearly, so large swathes of dialog are lost.  But enough remained to render it a very entertaining story.  There is to be a second series, I understand - will look forward to that.

B00BMKH5NW|5.0|As Cath struggles to assimilate to a college culture her sister Wren seems to dive into without hesitation, she's caught between two worlds: the fandom one in which she knows exactly how to maneuver, and real life, in which she feels hopelessly out of her depth.<br /><br />Like most new adult novels, we see the typical coming-of-age stories with boys, but they are less central to the story (and for this, I breathe a huge sigh of relief). Instead of being about ALL TEH SECKS, Cath's journey is about finding herself, not defining herself through a relationship with a boy. Boys are secondary. This is all about Cath.<br /><br />Best of all, Rowell totally gets the world of fandom. She has created a complex, Harry-Potter-like world that Cath's fan fiction exists in, and she walks the fine line of how unbelievably nerdy it can seem to outsiders while still showing how all-encompassing the world can become for some. Best of all, she acknowledges that great writers can exist in fandom -- and come out of it as well.<br /><br />This the the Kindle book with the most highlighting for me, and the one I probably have felt most intensely. For those of us are are -- and have been in the past -- a part of a fandom, Rowell has written OUR story. And it's fantastic.<br /><br />This review appeared previously on Goodreads.

B009POCG0Y|4.0|Tracy Letts' work typically portrays the lowest of the low in humanity and Killer Joe is no exception.  Because his work is so controversial and in-your-face, subtexts and even key overt elements are often overlooked.  For example, I have never read a single review that explores the reason Dottie is the way she is - she clearly suffered brain damage when her mother tried to asphyxiate her in her infancy, an incident she claims to actually remember.  In addition to the obvious disgust elicited by her dim-witted father's and \\"devoted\\" brother's willingness to have this fragile, virginal creature defiled by Joe as a \\"retainer\\", the brother's sexual desire for her is palpable.  One oddity is despite the fact people are shown vomiting, bleeding, and spitting on the floor, the trailer in which the main action takes place is and remains relatively neat and clean in appearance.  The actors all turn in genuine performances. If you've seen the play, you will never think of Killer Joe as anybody but Matthew McConaughey again.  While the other performances evoke emotions ranging from deep pity to utter disgust, McConaughey makes Killer Joe a person anybody in their right mind would fear from the moment they met him.  The violence is graphic, but believable.  We need to be reminded of the consequences of real violence sometimes lest we all succumb to the GTA mentality that it's all just a game we could go out and replicate in real life without harming anybody or anything - a mentality that may have resulted in recent mass shootings at malls, movie theaters, workplaces, etc.  If you want to see a film that moves you on an organic level, see this.  If you are easily offended by representations of human debris, don't.

B00BEIYP1W|4.0|Ron Howard's Rush succeeds on two levels, as a compelling story for the non-race fan and sufficiently accurate for the Formula 1 fan.<br /><br />The film is about the fierce competition between two Formula 1 drivers, James Hunt and Nikki Lauda. The central piece of the film is the 1976 racing season where Nikki Lauda is badly burned in a horrific crash. The two drivers are polar opposites, Hunt loved fast cars, fast women, and a fast life; Lauda was the technician who worked hard and was serious about everything. The differences between the two make for a fantastic drama.<br /><br />The casting was perfect. Chris Hemsworth (Thor) plays James Hunt the perfect combination Val Kilmer, Jim Morrison, and sex symbol. Daniel Br&uuml;hl (Bourne Ultimatum and Inglorious Basterds) does a magnificent job as the calculating and angered Nikki Lauda.<br /><br />This is a review of the theatrical release.<br /><br />Ron Howard and screenwriter Peter Morgan knew nothing about Formula 1 before they embarked on this film. I was convinced this film would go the Days Of Thunder direction. Thankfully they spent a lot of time with Nikki Lauda and some other drivers to get things closer to right. They still missed at times; the film isn't perfect from a racing perspective. They did a good enough job to keep the film entertaining without going crazy on racing details. Lauda has endorsed this film.<br /><br />Ron Howard appropriately paid homage to Grand Prix (there were several shots that looked exactly like James Garner in the 1960's car) and Le Mans; two of the greatest racing films ever made. This film concentrated more on the dramatic on track conflict between Hunt and Lauda and less on the backdrop love story that bogged down Grand Prix. Howard did not fall in the massive bear trap of over juicing the racing footage - Days Of Thunder and Driven being the worst racing films ever made. He did misstep a few times, drawing in a bit of NASCAR with two cars banging wheels. In Formula 1, open wheel racing, those two cars would have flown off the track and not just bounced off each other. There were a few moments where cars flew past each other, another Hollywood trick that doesn't ever happen in a real race. Other than those small things, the racing was well done. They bolted cameras all over the cars, including inches above the ground from underneath. In a day when there are constant in car cameras during actual races, it is hard to come up with new angles for racing. Rush found those new different angles.<br /><br />I liked the mix of historic footage and similar new footage that had an old school heavy grain to it. They did a masterful job blending those elements. Surprisingly the film was shot exclusively in Germany and the UK. Through editing and careful dressing they managed to make that footage look like tracks all over the world. They didn't do very well with Monaco, I'm not sure where they shot those short segments, but something was wrong with them. The cars from 1976 were beautiful. It was so wonderful to see those cars on a track running again. The sound was incredible - the rasp of those engines was superb.<br /><br />The film is rated R for nudity, language, and drugs. Strong language is used a lot in the film. The nudity is brief glimpses of women's breasts and full rear nudity of Chris Hemsworth (which would have passed for late prime time television). Drugs use is shown several times. There were several scenes about 2/3 of the way through the film that I couldn't bear to watch. Lauda's crash - I knew it was coming, I knew which race it happened at; but knowing didn't prepare me for how brutal it was and how it was depicted on the screen. I honestly, closed my eyes at that moment. I couldn't bear to watch the scenes in the hospital with the bandages and his lungs being vacuumed out. For those reasons, this is probably a film best left to mature audiences.<br /><br />Rush was an exciting film that I enjoyed a lot.eel racing, those two cars would have flown off the track and not just bounced off each other. 0307271609|5.0|Few rulers from Imperial China cast as long a shadow nor suffer from as much infamy as the Empress Dowager Cixi.  An extremely clever and resourceful woman, Cixi by dint of bearing the emperor a son and her own ability to seize any and all opportunities, rapidly moved up from low level concubine to top level concubine to Dowager Empress after the death of the Emperor in 1861.  Cixi and the wife of the late Emperor Xianfeng, Empress Zhen, engineered a coup with the aid of several Princes and allies.  Cixi proceeded to be the ruling force behind the throne for the next 47 years.  This was amazing for many reasons, not the least of which being the extremely patriarchal nature of Chinese society then.<br /><br />However, history has not been kind to Cixi, who these days is more known for her notorious behaviour - long fingernails (a sign of not having to work), daily meals of 100 courses or more, spending the entire budget for a navy in building her marble boat in the Summer Palace outside of Beijing, and resisting reforms leading to the collapse of the Qing dynast.<br /><br />Author Jung Chang sets out to correct this view in a well-researched and well-written biography of Cixi.  She presents a revisionist view of the Empress Dowager as an fair ruler and reformer.  By peeling back the layers of the onion, and using primary and secondary sources, Chang is able to develop a better and more nuanced portrait of Cixi.  Yes, she was ambitious, but rather than the anti-Westerner that stories have lead us to believe, she was quite interested in the rest of the world (in contrast to the previous emperors whose arrogance provoked the wrath of European and American governments).  Indeed, she sent delegations abroad, promoted learning in China beyond the traditional Confucian classics, and sought to improve the lot of her country.  However, as powerful as she was, there were many entrenched interests who wanted to restrict Chinese industrialization and development.  Ultimately, she was not able to prevent the disintegration of the Qing dynasty.<br /><br />Chang has written a fascinating book, starting with the background of Cixi, and then as she blossoms into a ruler during a tumultuous time.  This engaging effort to rehabilitate Cixi's reputation shows us that this complex woman did much good for China.e was not able to prevent the disintegration of the Qing dynasty.<br /><br />Chang has written a fascinating book, starting with the background of Cixi, and then as she blossoms into a ruler during a tumultuous time. If you are going to make a world in the United States, you better make your readers believe there was a plausible reason for the development of this strange world.<br /><br />The main elements of the world are:<br />1.  Love is evil.  Let's surgically remove it.<br />2.  We all live in big walled cities.<br />3.  Our religion tells us that the people who are saved are those who don't give into the disease of love.<br /><br />Love is evil.  I don't know if a big war has decimated the world or what, but it was determined if love was removed there would be a lot less war.  People of the United States, we have decided for your own good to perform brain surgery on you and remove the disease of love.  Never again will you love your children or spouse, nor will they love you in return.  I can't see anyone lining up for this surgery, except for the mentally ill.  WHAT ABOUT THE OTHER COUNTRIES?  If the U.S. has an emotional lobotomy, how will this stop other countries from going to war against us?  It doesn't.  If you think too hard, you realize that this element makes no sense.  All it would do is further divide the citizens of the United States and create a civil war.<br /><br />We all live in big walled cities.  It sounds like an urban prison camp.  For some unknown reason, people willingly gave up their homes, businesses, and farms in order to live in a walled urban camp.  You aren't allowed to leave.  How would you sell this to make people willingly do it?  In order to save you from war, you must give up all of your possessions and come live in a walled urban prison.  I don't think many people would willingly sign up for this.  And if everyone was so for this, why would they have to be moved into big urban camps?  To save you and your property from wars, we will give you brain surgery, take away your freedom, and take away your property.<br /><br />There is a new religion.  The United States is a largely Christian nation, and the leaders are mostly people who say they are Christians.  No reason is given why we would allow our religion to be changed.  It is clear that religion is still important to the people, so it makes zero sense why these same people would allow the government to change their religion to suit their own needs.  You can only be saved and go to heaven if you have the disease cut out.  God has spoken.<br /><br />I awarded zero stars for a plausible world.  The author just put in her own 'cool' elements, and she failed to make them believable.<br /><br />Lena's character was very carefully crafted and explained to us by the author.  It was made clear that she was DETERMINED to never catch the disease because she had a very difficult childhood living with her diseased mother.  Her mother ultimately killed herself.  She also had a difficult time at school because of her mother.  People like her were ostracized due to having relatives that went crazy from the disease.  Lena was looking forward to being cured, and she was extremely negative about anyone who did not act in the proper manner.  The author has given us a strong character who is strongly motivated to be a good citizen.<br /><br />Alex was super hot and he winked at her, so Lena does an about face on all her moral beliefs.  She breaks rules.  She falls in love.  Alex is an uncured who is hot.  Lena could hardly believe he was interested in her because she wasn't hot.  Other than Alex being hot, Lena had no motivation to break the rules.  So the author provides this piece of repulsive motivation, all her matches are ugly wimps.  Oh why do these books continually do this?  He's super hot, so I am a totally new girl...*excuse me while I vomit in my mouth*.<br /><br />Then to make the love match seem even more unlikely, the author carefully explains that when one member of the family suffers from love that the entire family will be punished for it.  Lena willingly throws her family to the wolves in order to feel love...unh!<br /><br />I award this zero stars.  I hated the characters.<br /><br />While the author had a nice writing style, I couldn't buy into anything in this book.  So two stars for a nice writing style.

0440237688|4.0|What would it be like to live in a world where everything his ordered and dictated by a manipulative hierarchy? Where your meals are brought to you daily and your job is predetermined and assigned to you at twelve? The Giver by Lois Lowry tackles this question in more in a tantalizingly thought provoking novel.<br /><br />Jonas's world is perfect. Everything is under control, and there are no choice. Fear, pain, love, and war. All have been eradicated as a sense of \\"Sameness\\" is being strived for in the Community. But when Jonas turns twelve, he, chosen from amongst all the other children, is assigned the prestigious role of \\"The Receiver of Memories.\\" He is to receive special training from The Giver. The Giver holds memories of the true pains and pleasures of life. He alone is burdened with this task as the rest of the Community remains oblivious. Colors do not exist, singing does not exist, love does not exist. But now it is time for Jonas to become the receiver of the truth. For him, there is no turning back.<br /><br />Lowry uses well-crafted prose to create this image of a dystopian world masked with a facade of utopia. It is thoughtful and well worth a read through or two. It is a good starting block to anyone interested in dystopian literature although its deep themes and intensive emotional level will require a slightly older, more mature reader--especially to appreciate the author's talent and the message.<br /><br />While it may be hard to grasp the concept of such a different lifestyle (and for some it may be hard to sit through), it is important to remember, Lowry is not advocating this flawed society. In fact, she is only inviting us to consider if our society shares any similarities with the flaws of The Community. Do we erase memories too painful to bear? What extent to we go to ensure equality? Do we place stigma against things that go against how things are supposed to be done?<br /><br />Ironically, this book has often been a subject of controversyand has found a place on banned book lists. Celebrate the freedom to read the just passed Banned Books Week and read a book as wonderful as The Giver. I would not find it to be a regrettable experience.

0307454568|5.0|I finally finished the last of Larsson's trilogy.  I was impressed with all three as I expected endless carnage from seeing snippets of the films and instead found a very well crafted and complex story, complete with complex and unforgettable characters.  The third book starts with everyone in bad shape so things can only improve. Lisbeth recovers from the incredible wounds inflicted on her by her family and prepares for trial.  One of Larsson's themes is that we are all responsible until the last of our days for our deeds and institutions so a large number of greybearded pensioners appear, both as good guys and bad.<br /><br />Some story lines seem un-neccessary (why would Berger have a stalker? how did Lisbeth's lawyer end up in Spain) but they are useful to tie things out and even introduce a few new characters, morals, and approaches. The tragedy is we will never really know what Larsson planned next for us, as he did not live to take on another book. What does the future hold for our journalist with all the girlfriends, the magazine, and of course, Lisbeth?  Fortunately everyone ends up working together in the end, even the press and the police.  Not sure that was necessary but it helps to wrap things up.  Larsson is such a good writer that he ties up loose ends throughout the book, not waiting until the last 10 pages like some of his lesser competitors.<br /><br />Larsson has made this trilogy somewhat autobiographical, although I dont know if he is so polished with the ladies.  We learn some important lessons about ethics, here delivered through the prism of journalists.
